1781162646|5.0|Joyland consumed me, took me down into a world I can still remember outside of the novel because I lived through those times as well, and left me nostalgic and emotionally exhausted.  This book is one of Stephen King's best, a steady, haunting melody of memory and youth and innocence that was euphoric.<br /><br />Readers looking for one of King's bloodthirsty romps aren't going to get that here.  They're going to get the Stephen King that wrote Bag of Bones, Hearts in Atlantis, and \\"The Body,\\" all tales that I enjoy and recommend to any reader, especially those who think that all the author produces are horror stories.<br /><br />Devin Jones, the main character, is me at 21, and I think he's a lot of the guys who grew up in the 1970s when the Vietnam War was going on, the battle of the sexes was being waged, and the world was in turmoil.  King doesn't really talk about the big issues in this novel, but you can see them in Dev is you know where to look.<br /><br />Dev is a guy who's just trying to live a quiet life, a \\"right\\" life, and find a small amount of happiness chasing his dreams.  Of course, he's chasing after the wrong girl, and yeah, a lot of us have been there too.<br /><br />The book wanders around a lot and introduces a lot of characters, but I was glad to check out the scenery and I enjoyed getting to meet all the people.  There are several familiar characters in these pages, all of them the kind of folks King generally tucks into his stories - real people with real histories that we only get glimpses of, which is like real life too.  You get the feeling that King knows a lot more about these characters than he shows here, and with his habit of bringing characters back to sprinkle into other novels, I get the feeling that we'll see some of them again.<br /><br />Of course, we won't see some of them again.  That's one of the things that King does too:  introduces us to people then tells us how they were lost along the way.  He uses a lot of introspective storytelling in this novel, but it really works, although there are some times big changes in the timeline jar.<br /><br />The crux of the book is the murder that took place in Joyland's Horror House, but that's just the linchpin that frames Dev's story.  After a bitter breakup with his college sweetheart, he's looking for something to focus his attentions on and the mysterious murdered girl whose ghost sometimes appears to Joyland patrons provides enough of a mystery to entice him - and the readers.<br /><br />It's really hard to qualify this book.  It's kind of a ghost story.  It's kind of a mystery.  It's kind of a coming of age novel.<br /><br />But it's the best kind of Stephen King novel.  I read it during a drizzly night that unraveled into the small hours of morning, the perfect time to remember those vulnerable years and to believe in things that bump ever so gently in the dark corners of the world.storytelling in this novel, but it really works, although there are some times big changes in the timeline jar.<br /><br />The crux of the book is the murder that took place in Joyland's Horror House, but that's just the linchpin that frames Dev's story. Pretty slick.<br /><br />Northern Borders consists of excellent beats and luscious samples layered within a forest of percussion and exotic horn. 0375869026|5.0|Wonder introduces us to August (\\"Auggie\\"), a 10 year-old boy who is born with a horribly disfigured face.  Homeschooled his whole life, his parents finally make the decision to send Auggie to 5th grade in the local public school.  What follows is an incredible story of family, friendship, and the triumph of humanity and kindness.<br /><br />Every now and then, a book moves me.  Wonder is that kind of book - the kind where you run into other parents in the school yard, you chat with your children's teachers, you call up your friends and family with kids and say:  YOU HAVE TO READ THIS BOOK.  Everyone should read this book.  No, wait... every middle grade school should read this book.  It should be part of middle grade curriculum.  No, everyone should read this book.  There's a lesson here for everyone.<br /><br />Auggie is one of the most endearing main characters I have run across in a very long time.  As he is introduced, we do not know exactly what is wrong with Auggie or why.  This is what the author does so well, in revealing his disfigurement, providing only small glimpses so that we get to know the real Auggie before we can only see him as horribly disfigured.  Of course, we have that luxury as readers, but those who would encounter Auggie in person would see his disfigurement right away; thus, as readers, we have a very different experience in getting to know Auggie.<br /><br />Based on what we know about bullying in schools, we would anticipate that a child like Auggie would be a magnet to being abused, mocked, or excluded.  This DOES happen, but what is so beautiful is that there are many other children who look past Auggie's \\"face\\" and see what a \\"wonder\\"-ful person he is on the inside - something we see as readers all along.  Additionally, these children go a step further to stand up to the bully and choose their own path.  This is so well done!<br /><br />And then there is Mr. Browne, the English teacher, and his precepts.  Each month the children are giving a precept (i.e., \\"motto\\", \\"quote\\", etc.) to ponder and write an essay about.  While there are many noteworthy ones I can highlight, it is the first that is the most poignant:<br /><br />  \\"WHEN GIVEN THE CHOICE BETWEEN BEING RIGHT OR BEING KIND, CHOOSE KIND\\"<br /><br />I just about had a heart attack when I read that!  I have been living by those EXACT words for 20 years after I first encountered a similar quote in the 1950s movie Harvey starring Jimmy Stewart (by the way, there are many, many fabulous quotes in that movie).  This is the quote from the movie:<br /><br />  \\"Years ago my mother used to say to me, she'd say, 'In this world, Elwood, you must be' - she always called me Elwood - 'In this world, Elwood, you must be oh so smart or oh so pleasant.' Well, for years I was smart. I recommend pleasant. You may quote me.<br /><br />Same idea right?!  So, although there are other equally relevant precepts, this is the one that will stay with the reader because it is so simple:  We can choose kind.  As I was reading through the book, I was thinking to myself, \\"This is not going to end well.\\"  I was oh-so-wrong and I can say that at the end, tears streaming down my face I felt HOPE that if just given the words, given the options, children CAN choose kind.<br /><br />I honestly can't rave enough about this book.<br /><br />One note however ... my 10 year-old daughter read this book as well.  She did enjoy it, but I really sensed that it underwhelmed her.  I think that at her age, there is an expectation that a book is purely entertaining.  That is, books should be filled with fantasy, magic, adventure, alternate realities, and so on.  This is not THAT kind of book.  This is REAL.  This is a book that elicits 101 great discussions and that is why I think it she wasn't over-the-moon about it the same way I was. B006XOBFJC|3.0|Sony's 14-disc re-release of Marek Janowski's \\"Ring of the Nibelungen\\" is pretty good throughout but no better.  The cycle was recorded in the early 1980s in East Germany and features a number of important and talented vocalists.  Clocking in at just over 14 hours, I'll make some comments on summary areas, starting with the good.<br /><br />Theo Adam and Rene Kollo::  Theo sings the role of Wotan and Kollo does Siegfried.  They are excellent throughout in what is a taxing and consistently difficult pair of parts.  Kollo has a big range and sings the higher passages with accuracy and a lack of audible effort.  Adam, a baritone who made this recording in the twilight of his career, was well-known for his Wagner interpretations and I think makes the best individual effort on this entire set, an important plus given how central the roles of Wotan and his disguised twin The Wanderer are to the Ring cycle.<br /><br />Marek Janowski: The Ring is a cycle often dominated by the orchestra and so the role of the conductor is key to any performance.  How does Janowski do?  I'd say \\"ok\\" but no better.  The Staatskapelle Dresden plays accurately throughout but Janowski misses many opportunities for beauty.  To take one example, the exciting opening of \\"Siegfried\\" Act III, leading up to a dialogue between The Wanderer and Erda, the spirit of the earth, is played without the intensity we know it can possess.  Likewise, two of the most beautiful orchestral depictions in the Ring, the opening Prelude of \\"Das Rheingold\\" and Siegfried's Funeral Music in the last act of \\"Gotterdammerung\\", are simply not as beautiful and striking as I've heard elsewhere.  I attribute this not to incompetence - the orchestra plays in a technically assured way throughout - but to a simple lack of inspiration and intensity on Janowski's part.  To be fair, I do want to point to one section where the orchestra contributes particularly effectively: the gloomy forest scene initiating Act II of \\"Siegfried\\" is done perfectly, somber and spooky.<br /><br />The sound: This is a problem.  The cycle is an early digital effort done using a slightly different type of recording technology.  I found the midrange to be very good but the bass is boomy and the treble, particularly problematic, is full of hash and lacks clarity.  I don't know whether to attribute this to the original technology or the re-mastering, but whatever the source of the problems, a Ring cycle disadvantaging Wagner's spectacular orchestration - he is for me one of the best orchestrators in history - and making harsh the already difficult higher vocal passages - well... it's a problem.<br /><br />I need to at least mention the performance of Jeannine Altmeyer, in the lead female role of Brunnhilde.  Altmeyer, in the early part of her career, is pretty good, very musical in the passages where she feels comfortable but a bit out of control in the extremely hard ones.<br /><br />This is somewhat harsh commentary on a cycle that won a Grammy when first released.  The three stars reflect the major issues with any Ring cycle featuring a middling orchestral performance and mediocre sound engineering.  Before writing this review, I asked myself whether in the future I'd listen to it when I wanted to hear the Ring, rather than my reference Georg Solti cycle and the answer was a flat - with the exception of that powerful Act II from Siegfried - \\"no way.\\"ne perfectly, somber and spooky.<br /><br />The sound: This is a problem. While there are a few things I miss, like some louder guitar for one, I don't miss the preciousness of the first album. This is a band that no longer squeals \\"college band\\" at every turn of phrase or overtly and obvious attention grabbing musical stunt. The Vampire Weekend of the debut would probably not be telling you \\"There's a headstone right in front of you\\" (\\"Don't Lie\\"), for example.<br /><br />There are still a few plays for the radio. \\"Diane Young\\" clips along at a kinetic pace and even throws in some auto-tune to mock anyone who wants to call \\"Modern Vampires\\" 'serious music,' all the while playing pun games with \\"Diane Young\\" and 'dying young.' It also contains one of the weirder lyrical choices I've heard on a record this year, Koenig tells Diane she's got \\"the luck of a Kennedy.\\" Yes, they want to be taken seriously with \\"Modern Vampires Of The City\\" taking on life, death, religion and the big bad specter of growing up, but Vampire Weekend has that cake and their quirky, too.
